<div style="line-height: 1.6em; font-weight: bold; font-size: 100%; text-align: left;"><div>Influenced</div></div>
<ul class="mw-collapsible-content" style="font-size: 105%; margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0; line-height: inherit; text-align: left; list-style: none; margin-left: 0;"><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Virtually all of subsequent <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Catholic_theology" title="Catholic theology">Catholic theology</a>.</div></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Thomas Aquinas</b>, <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">OP</a> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'w' in 'wind'">w</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>: <i lang="it">Tommaso d'Aquino</i>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'Thomas of <a href="/wiki/Aquino,_Italy" title="Aquino, Italy">Aquino</a>'; 1225 – 7 March 1274) was an Italian<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway1911_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway1911-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaughan1871_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaughan1871-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican</a> <a href="/wiki/Friar" title="Friar">friar</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_priest" title="Catholic priest">priest</a>, who was an immensely influential <a href="/wiki/List_of_Catholic_philosophers_and_theologians" title="List of Catholic philosophers and theologians">philosopher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catholic_theology" title="Catholic theology">theologian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jurist" title="Jurist">jurist</a> in the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">scholasticism</a>; he is known within the scholastic tradition as the <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Doctor Angelicus</i></span>, the <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Doctor Communis</i></span>, and the <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Doctor Universalis</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pius_XI_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pius_XI-11">[a]</a></sup> The name <i>Aquinas</i> identifies his ancestral origins in the county of <a href="/wiki/Aquino,_Italy" title="Aquino, Italy">Aquino</a> in present-day <a href="/wiki/Lazio" title="Lazio">Lazio</a>, Italy. Among other things, he was a prominent proponent of <a href="/wiki/Natural_theology" title="Natural theology">natural theology</a> and the father of a school of thought (encompassing both theology and philosophy) known as <a href="/wiki/Thomism" title="Thomism">Thomism</a>. He argued that God is the source of both the light of natural reason and the light of faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> He has been described as "the most influential thinker of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_period" title="Medieval period">medieval period</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> and "the greatest of the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_philosophy" title="Medieval philosophy">medieval philosopher</a>-theologians".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> His influence on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_thought" title="Western thought">Western thought</a> is considerable, and much of <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a> is derived from his ideas, particularly in the areas of ethics, <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, and political theory.   
</p><p>Unlike many currents in the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> of the time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP3_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEP3-15">[14]</a></sup> Thomas embraced several ideas put forward by <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>—whom he called "the Philosopher"—and attempted to synthesize <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian philosophy</a> with the principles of Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>His best-known works are the <i>Disputed Questions on Truth</i> (1256–1259), the <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_contra_Gentiles" title="Summa contra Gentiles">Summa contra Gentiles</a></i> (1259–1265), and the unfinished but massively influential <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa Theologica</a></i>, or <i>Summa Theologiae</i> (1265–1274). His commentaries on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scripture" title="Scripture">Scripture</a> and on Aristotle also form an important part of his body of work. Furthermore, Thomas is distinguished for his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eucharistic" title="Eucharistic">eucharistic</a> hymns, which form a part of the church's liturgy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> The Catholic Church honors Thomas Aquinas as a <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saint</a> and regards him as the model teacher for those studying for the priesthood, and indeed the highest expression of both natural reason and <a href="/wiki/Speculative_reason" title="Speculative reason">speculative</a> <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>. In modern times, under papal directives, the study of his works was long used as a core of the required program of study for those seeking ordination as priests or deacons, as well as for those in religious formation and for other students of the sacred disciplines (philosophy, Catholic theology, church history, liturgy, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canon_law_(Catholic_Church)" title="Canon law (Catholic Church)">canon law</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>As a <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_the_Church" title="Doctor of the Church">Doctor of the Church</a>, Thomas Aquinas is considered one of the Catholic Church's greatest theologians and philosophers. <a href="/wiki/Pope_Benedict_XV" title="Pope Benedict XV">Pope Benedict XV</a> declared: "This (Dominican) Order<span class="nowrap"> </span>... acquired new luster when the Church declared the teaching of Thomas to be her own and that Doctor, honored with the special praises of the Pontiffs, the master and patron of Catholic schools."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Early_life_.281225.E2.80.931244.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_(1225–1244)">Early life (1225–1244)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life (1225–1244)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Thomas Aquinas was most likely born in the castle of <a href="/wiki/Roccasecca" title="Roccasecca">Roccasecca</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Aquino,_Italy" title="Aquino, Italy">Aquino</a>, controlled at that time by the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sicily" title="Kingdom of Sicily">Kingdom of Sicily</a> (in present-day <a href="/wiki/Lazio" title="Lazio">Lazio</a>, Italy), <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1225</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> According to some authors,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (November 2015)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> he was born in the castle of his father, Landulf of Aquino. He was born to the most powerful branch of the family, and Landulf of Aquino was a man of means. As a knight in the service of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Frederick_II" title="Emperor Frederick II">Emperor Frederick II</a>, Landulf of Aquino held the title <i><a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">miles</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> Thomas's mother, Theodora, belonged to the Rossi branch of the <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Neapolitan</a> Caracciolo family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorrell20053_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorrell20053-23">[22]</a></sup> Landulf's brother Sinibald was <a href="/wiki/Abbot" title="Abbot">abbot</a> of <a href="/wiki/Monte_Cassino" title="Monte Cassino">Monte Cassino</a>, the oldest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Order_of_St._Benedictine" title="Order of St. Benedictine">Benedictine</a> <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monastery</a>. While the rest of the family's sons pursued military careers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHampden184814_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHampden184814-24">[23]</a></sup> the family intended for Thomas to follow his uncle into the abbacy;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStump20033_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStump20033-25">[24]</a></sup> this would have been a normal career path for a younger son of southern Italian nobility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schaff-422_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-schaff-422-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the age of five Thomas began his early education at Monte Cassino, but after the military conflict between the Emperor Frederick II and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_IX" title="Pope Gregory IX">Pope Gregory IX</a> spilled into the abbey in early 1239, Landulf and Theodora had Thomas enrolled at the <i><a href="/wiki/Studium_generale" title="Studium generale">studium generale</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/University_of_Naples_Federico_II" title="University of Naples Federico II">university</a>) recently established by Frederick in <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies20041–2_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies20041–2-27">[26]</a></sup> There his teacher in arithmetic, geometry, astronomy, and music was <a href="/wiki/Petrus_de_Ibernia" title="Petrus de Ibernia">Petrus de Ibernia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrabmann19632_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrabmann19632-28">[27]</a></sup> It was here that Thomas was probably introduced to <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a>, all of whom would influence his theological philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies20042_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies20042-29">[28]</a></sup> It was also during his study at Naples that Thomas came under the influence of John of St. Julian, a Dominican preacher in Naples, who was part of the active effort by the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican Order</a> to recruit devout followers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHampden184821–22_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHampden184821–22-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Castello_di_Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano_9.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="639" data-file-width="852" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Castello_di_Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano_9.JPG/220px-Castello_di_Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano_9.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Castello_di_Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano_9.JPG/330px-Castello_di_Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano_9.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Castello_di_Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano_9.JPG/440px-Castello_di_Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano_9.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Castello_di_Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano_9.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Castle of <a href="/wiki/Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano" title="Monte San Giovanni Campano">Monte San Giovanni Campano</a></div></div></div>
<p>At the age of nineteen Thomas resolved to join the Dominican Order, which had been founded about 30 years earlier. Thomas's change of heart did not please his family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> In an attempt to prevent Theodora's interference in Thomas's choice, the Dominicans arranged to move Thomas to Rome, and from Rome, to Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHampden184823_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHampden184823-32">[31]</a></sup> However, while on his journey to Rome, per Theodora's instructions, his brothers seized him as he was drinking from a spring and took him back to his parents at the castle of <a href="/wiki/Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano" title="Monte San Giovanni Campano">Monte San Giovanni Campano</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHampden184823_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHampden184823-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas was held prisoner for almost one year in the family castles at Monte San Giovanni and Roccasecca in an attempt to prevent him from assuming the Dominican habit and to push him into renouncing his new aspiration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies20042_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies20042-29">[28]</a></sup> Political concerns prevented the Pope from ordering Thomas's release, which had the effect of extending Thomas's detention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHampden184824_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHampden184824-33">[32]</a></sup> Thomas passed this time of trial tutoring his sisters and communicating with members of the Dominican Order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies20042_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies20042-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Family members became desperate to dissuade Thomas, who remained determined to join the Dominicans. At one point, two of his brothers resorted to the measure of hiring a prostitute to seduce him. As included in the official records for his canonization, Thomas drove her away wielding a burning log—with which he inscribed a cross onto the wall—and fell into a mystical ecstasy; two angels appeared to him as he slept and said, "Behold, we gird thee by the command of God with the girdle of chastity, which henceforth will never be imperiled. What human strength can not obtain, is now bestowed upon thee as a celestial gift." From that moment on, Thomas was given the grace of perfect chastity by Christ and he wore the girdle till the end of his life. The girdle was given to the ancient monastery of <a href="/wiki/Vercelli" title="Vercelli">Vercelli</a> in <a href="/wiki/Piedmont" title="Piedmont">Piedmont</a>, and is now at <a href="/wiki/Chieri" title="Chieri">Chieri</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">Turin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHampden184825_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHampden184825-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Thomas_Aquinas_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="956" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Saint_Thomas_Aquinas_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez.jpg/220px-Saint_Thomas_Aquinas_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Saint_Thomas_Aquinas_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez.jpg/330px-Saint_Thomas_Aquinas_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Saint_Thomas_Aquinas_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez.jpg/440px-Saint_Thomas_Aquinas_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Thomas_Aquinas_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thomas is girded by angels with a mystical belt of purity after his <a href="/wiki/Chastity" title="Chastity">proof of chastity</a>. Painting by <a href="/wiki/Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez" title="Diego Velázquez">Diego Velázquez</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>By 1244, seeing that all her attempts to dissuade Thomas had failed, Theodora sought to save the family's dignity, arranging for Thomas to escape at night through his window. In her mind, a secret escape from detention was less damaging than an open surrender to the Dominicans. Thomas was sent first to Naples and then to Rome to meet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johannes_von_Wildeshausen" title="Johannes von Wildeshausen">Johannes von Wildeshausen</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Master_General_of_the_Dominican_Order" title="Master General of the Dominican Order">Master General of the Dominican Order</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHampden184827–28_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHampden184827–28-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Paris.2C_Cologne.2C_Albert_Magnus.2C_and_first_Paris_regency_.281245.E2.80.931259.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Paris,_Cologne,_Albert_Magnus,_and_first_Paris_regency_(1245–1259)">Paris, Cologne, Albert Magnus, and first Paris regency (1245–1259)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Paris, Cologne, Albert Magnus, and first Paris regency (1245–1259)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1245, Thomas was sent to study at the Faculty of the Arts at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a>, where he most likely met Dominican scholar <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albertus Magnus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHealy20032_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHealy20032-37">[36]</a></sup> then the holder of the Chair of Theology at the College of St. James in Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHampden184833_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHampden184833-38">[37]</a></sup> When Albertus was sent by his superiors to teach at the new <i>studium generale</i> at Cologne in 1248,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHealy20032_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHealy20032-37">[36]</a></sup> Thomas followed him, declining <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_IV" title="Pope Innocent IV">Pope Innocent IV</a>'s offer to appoint him abbot of Monte Cassino as a Dominican.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStump20033_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStump20033-25">[24]</a></sup> Albertus then appointed the reluctant Thomas <i>magister studentium</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schaff-422_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-schaff-422-26">[25]</a></sup> Because Thomas was quiet and didn't speak much, some of his fellow students thought he was slow. But Albertus prophetically exclaimed: "You call him the dumb ox, but in his teaching he will one day produce such a bellowing that it will be heard throughout the world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStump20033_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStump20033-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas taught in Cologne as an apprentice professor (<i>baccalaureus biblicus</i>), instructing students on the books of the Old Testament and writing <i>Expositio super Isaiam ad litteram</i> (<i>Literal Commentary on Isaiah</i>), <i>Postilla super Ieremiam</i> (<i>Commentary on Jeremiah</i>) and <i>Postilla super Threnos</i> (<i>Commentary on Lamentations</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStump2003xvi_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStump2003xvi-39">[38]</a></sup> Then in 1252 he returned to Paris to study for the master's degree in theology. He lectured on the Bible as an apprentice professor, and upon becoming a <i>baccalaureus Sententiarum</i> (bachelor of the <i>Sentences</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies19935_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies19935-40">[39]</a></sup> he devoted his final three years of study to commenting on <a href="/wiki/Peter_Lombard" title="Peter Lombard">Peter Lombard</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Sentences" title="Sentences">Sentences</a></i>. In the first of his four theological syntheses, Thomas composed a massive commentary on the <i>Sentences</i> titled <i>Scriptum super libros Sententiarium</i> (<i>Commentary on the Sentences</i>). Aside from his master's writings, he wrote <i>De ente et essentia</i> (<i>On Being and Essence</i>) for his fellow Dominicans in Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStump20033_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStump20033-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the spring of 1256 Thomas was appointed regent master in theology at Paris and one of his first works upon assuming this office was <i>Contra impugnantes Dei cultum et religionem</i> (<i>Against Those Who Assail the Worship of God and Religion</i>), defending the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mendicant_order" title="Mendicant order">mendicant orders</a>, which had come under attack by <a href="/wiki/William_of_Saint-Amour" title="William of Saint-Amour">William of Saint-Amour</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> During his tenure from 1256 to 1259, Thomas wrote numerous works, including: <i>Questiones disputatae de veritate</i> (<i>Disputed Questions on Truth</i>), a collection of twenty-nine disputed questions on aspects of faith and the human condition<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStump20034_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStump20034-42">[41]</a></sup> prepared for the public university debates he presided over during <a href="/wiki/Lent" title="Lent">Lent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Advent" title="Advent">Advent</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies20043–4_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies20043–4-43">[42]</a></sup> <i>Quaestiones quodlibetales</i> (<i>Quodlibetal Questions</i>), a collection of his responses to questions posed to him by the academic audience;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStump20034_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStump20034-42">[41]</a></sup> and both <i>Expositio super librum Boethii De trinitate</i> (<i>Commentary on Boethius's De trinitate</i>) and <i>Expositio super librum Boethii De hebdomadibus</i> (<i>Commentary on Boethius's De hebdomadibus</i>), commentaries on the works of 6th-century Roman philosopher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anicius_Manlius_Severinus_Boethius" title="Anicius Manlius Severinus Boethius">Boethius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStump2003xvii_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStump2003xvii-44">[43]</a></sup> By the end of his regency, Thomas was working on one of his most famous works, <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_contra_Gentiles" title="Summa contra Gentiles">Summa contra Gentiles</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies20044_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies20044-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Naples.2C_Orvieto.2C_Rome_.281259.E2.80.931268.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Naples,_Orvieto,_Rome_(1259–1268)">Naples, Orvieto, Rome (1259–1268)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Naples, Orvieto, Rome (1259–1268)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1259, Thomas completed his first regency at the <i><a href="/wiki/Studium_generale" title="Studium generale">studium generale</a></i> and left Paris so that others in his order could gain this teaching experience. He returned to Naples where he was appointed as general preacher by the provincial chapter of 29 September 1260. In September 1261 he was called to <a href="/wiki/Orvieto" title="Orvieto">Orvieto</a>, where as conventual lector he was responsible for the pastoral formation of the friars unable to attend a <i>studium generale</i>. In Orvieto Thomas completed his <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_contra_Gentiles" title="Summa contra Gentiles">Summa contra Gentiles</a></i>, wrote the <i>Catena aurea</i> (<i>The Golden Chain</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHealy20034_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHealy20034-46">[45]</a></sup> and produced works for <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_IV" title="Pope Urban IV">Pope Urban IV</a> such as the liturgy for the newly created <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_Corpus_Christi" title="Feast of Corpus Christi">feast of Corpus Christi</a> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Contra_Errores_Graecorum" title="Contra Errores Graecorum">Contra errores graecorum</a></i> (<i>Against the Errors of the Greeks</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies20044_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies20044-45">[44]</a></sup> Some of the hymns that Thomas wrote for the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_Corpus_Christi" title="Feast of Corpus Christi">feast of Corpus Christi</a> are still sung today, such as the <i>Pange lingua</i> (whose penultimate verse is the famous <i>Tantum ergo</i>), and <i>Panis angelicus</i>. Modern scholarship has confirmed that Thomas was indeed the author of these texts, a point that some had contested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorrell2005129–32_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorrell2005129–32-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 1265 the newly elected <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_IV" title="Pope Clement IV">Pope Clement IV</a> summoned Thomas to Rome to serve as papal theologian. This same year he was ordered by the Dominican Chapter of Agnani<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> to teach at the <i><a href="/wiki/Medieval_university" title="Medieval university">studium</a></i> <i>conventuale</i> at the Roman <a href="/wiki/Convent" title="Convent">convent</a> of <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a>, founded some years before, in 1222.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-49">[48]</a></sup> The <i>studium</i> at <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a> now became an experiment for the Dominicans, the Order's first <i>studium provinciale</i>, an intermediate school between the <i>studium conventuale</i> and the <i>studium generale</i>. Prior to this time the Roman Province had offered no specialized education of any sort, no arts, no philosophy; only simple convent schools, with their basic courses in theology for resident friars, were functioning in Tuscany and the meridionale during the first several decades of the order's life. The new <i>studium provinciale</i> at <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a> was to be a more advanced school for the province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulchahey1998279_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulchahey1998279-50">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bartholomew_of_Lucca" title="Bartholomew of Lucca">Tolomeo da Lucca</a>, an associate and early biographer of Thomas, tells us that at the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a> <i>studium</i> Thomas taught the full range of philosophical subjects, both moral and natural.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>While at the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a> <i>studium provinciale</i> Thomas began his most famous work, the <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa Theologiae</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHealy20034_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHealy20034-46">[45]</a></sup> which he conceived specifically suited to beginning students: "Because a doctor of Catholic truth ought not only to teach the proficient, but to him pertains also to instruct beginners. As the Apostle says in 1 Corinthians 3:1–2, <i>as to infants in Christ, I gave you milk to drink, not meat</i>, our proposed intention in this work is to convey those things that pertain to the Christian religion in a way that is fitting to the instruction of beginners."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> While there he also wrote a variety of other works like his unfinished <i>Compendium Theologiae</i> and <i>Responsio ad fr. Ioannem Vercellensem de articulis 108 sumptis ex opere Petri de Tarentasia</i> (<i>Reply to Brother John of Vercelli Regarding 108 Articles Drawn from the Work of Peter of Tarentaise</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStump2003xvii_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStump2003xvii-44">[43]</a></sup> In his position as head of the <i>studium</i> Thomas conducted a series of important disputations on the power of God, which he compiled into his <i>De potentia</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies20045_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies20045-53">[52]</a></sup> Nicholas Brunacci [1240–1322] was among Thomas's students at the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a> <i>studium provinciale</i> and later at the Paris <i>studium generale</i>. In November 1268 he was with Thomas and his associate and secretary <a href="/wiki/Reginald_of_Piperno" title="Reginald of Piperno">Reginald of Piperno</a>, as they left Viterbo on their way to Paris to begin the academic year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> Another student of Thomas's at the Santa Sabina <i>studium provinciale</i> was <a href="/wiki/Beatification" title="Beatification">Blessed</a> Tommasello da Perugia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas remained at the <i>studium</i> at <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a> from 1265 until he was called back to Paris in 1268 for a second teaching regency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies20045_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies20045-53">[52]</a></sup> With his departure for Paris in 1268 and the passage of time the pedagogical activities of the <i>studium provinciale</i> at <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a> were divided between two campuses. A new <a href="/wiki/Convent" title="Convent">convent</a> of the Order at the Church of <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_sopra_Minerva" title="Santa Maria sopra Minerva">Santa Maria sopra Minerva</a> had a modest beginning in 1255 as a community for women converts, but grew rapidly in size and importance after being given over to the Dominicans friars in 1275.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-49">[48]</a></sup> In 1288 the theology component of the provincial curriculum for the education of the friars was relocated from the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a> <i>studium provinciale</i> to the <i>studium conventuale</i> at <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_sopra_Minerva" title="Santa Maria sopra Minerva">Santa Maria sopra Minerva</a>, which was redesignated as a <i>studium particularis theologiae</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMulchahey1998323_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMulchahey1998323-57">[56]</a></sup> This <i>studium</i> was transformed in the 16th century into the College of Saint Thomas (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Collegium Divi Thomæ</i>). In the 20th century the college was relocated to the convent of <a href="/wiki/Santi_Domenico_e_Sisto" title="Santi Domenico e Sisto">Saints Dominic and Sixtus</a> and was transformed into the <a href="/wiki/Pontifical_University_of_Saint_Thomas_Aquinas" title="Pontifical University of Saint Thomas Aquinas">Pontifical University of Saint Thomas Aquinas, <i>Angelicum</i></a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Quarrelsome_second_Paris_regency_.281269.E2.80.931272.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Quarrelsome_second_Paris_regency_(1269–1272)">Quarrelsome second Paris regency (1269–1272)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Quarrelsome second Paris regency (1269–1272)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benozzo_Gozzoli_-_Triumph_of_St_Thomas_Aquinas_-_WGA10334.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1293" data-file-width="570" decoding="async" height="340" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Benozzo_Gozzoli_-_Triumph_of_St_Thomas_Aquinas_-_WGA10334.jpg/150px-Benozzo_Gozzoli_-_Triumph_of_St_Thomas_Aquinas_-_WGA10334.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Benozzo_Gozzoli_-_Triumph_of_St_Thomas_Aquinas_-_WGA10334.jpg/225px-Benozzo_Gozzoli_-_Triumph_of_St_Thomas_Aquinas_-_WGA10334.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Benozzo_Gozzoli_-_Triumph_of_St_Thomas_Aquinas_-_WGA10334.jpg/300px-Benozzo_Gozzoli_-_Triumph_of_St_Thomas_Aquinas_-_WGA10334.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Benozzo_Gozzoli_-_Triumph_of_St_Thomas_Aquinas_-_WGA10334.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Triumph of St Thomas Aquinas</i>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Doctor Communis</i></span>, between Plato and Aristotle, <a href="/wiki/Benozzo_Gozzoli" title="Benozzo Gozzoli">Benozzo Gozzoli</a>, 1471. Louvre, Paris.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1268 the Dominican Order assigned Thomas to be regent master at the University of Paris for a second time, a position he held until the spring of 1272. Part of the reason for this sudden reassignment appears to have arisen from the rise of "<a href="/wiki/Averroism" title="Averroism">Averroism</a>" or "radical <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelianism</a>" in the universities. In response to these perceived errors, Thomas wrote two works, one of them being <i>De unitate intellectus, contra Averroistas</i> (<i>On the Unity of Intellect, against the Averroists</i>) in which he reprimands Averroism as incompatible with Christian doctrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStump200310–11_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStump200310–11-58">[57]</a></sup> During his second regency, he finished the second part of the <i>Summa</i> and wrote <i>De virtutibus</i> and <i>De aeternitate mundi, contra murmurantes</i> (<i>On the Eternity of the World, against Grumblers</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies20045_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies20045-53">[52]</a></sup> the latter of which dealt with controversial Averroist and Aristotelian <i>beginninglessness</i> of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStump200311–12_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStump200311–12-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Eternity_of_the_world" title="Eternity of the world">Eternity of the world</a></div>
<p>Disputes with some important <a href="/wiki/Franciscans" title="Franciscans">Franciscans</a> conspired to make his second regency much more difficult and troubled than the first. A year before Thomas re-assumed the regency at the 1266–67 Paris disputations, Franciscan master William of Baglione accused Thomas of encouraging Averroists, most likely counting him as one of the "blind leaders of the blind". <a href="/wiki/Eleonore_Stump" title="Eleonore Stump">Eleonore Stump</a> says, "It has also been persuasively argued that Thomas Aquinas's <i>De aeternitate mundi</i> was directed in particular against his Franciscan colleague in theology, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Pecham" title="John Pecham">John Pecham</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStump200311–12_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStump200311–12-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>In reality, Thomas was deeply disturbed by the spread of Averroism and was angered when he discovered <a href="/wiki/Siger_of_Brabant" title="Siger of Brabant">Siger of Brabant</a> teaching Averroistic interpretations of Aristotle to Parisian students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> On 10 December 1270, the Bishop of Paris, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Tempier" title="Étienne Tempier">Étienne Tempier</a>, issued an edict condemning thirteen Aristotelian and Averroistic propositions as heretical and excommunicating anyone who continued to support them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> Many in the ecclesiastical community, the so-called Augustinians, were fearful that this introduction of Aristotelianism and the more extreme Averroism might somehow contaminate the purity of the Christian faith. In what appears to be an attempt to counteract the growing fear of Aristotelian thought, Thomas conducted a series of disputations between 1270 and 1272: <i>De virtutibus in communi</i> (<i>On Virtues in General</i>), <i>De virtutibus cardinalibus</i> (<i>On Cardinal Virtues</i>), and <i>De spe</i> (<i>On Hope</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Late_career_and_cessation_of_writing_.281272.E2.80.931274.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_career_and_cessation_of_writing_(1272–1274)">Late career and cessation of writing (1272–1274)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Late career and cessation of writing (1272–1274)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Patrick_Church_(Columbus,_Ohio)_-_stained_glass,_St._Thomas_Aquinas,_detail.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3587" data-file-width="2017" decoding="async" height="356" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Saint_Patrick_Church_%28Columbus%2C_Ohio%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg/200px-Saint_Patrick_Church_%28Columbus%2C_Ohio%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Saint_Patrick_Church_%28Columbus%2C_Ohio%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg/300px-Saint_Patrick_Church_%28Columbus%2C_Ohio%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Saint_Patrick_Church_%28Columbus%2C_Ohio%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg/400px-Saint_Patrick_Church_%28Columbus%2C_Ohio%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Patrick_Church_(Columbus,_Ohio)_-_stained_glass,_St._Thomas_Aquinas,_detail.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Icon of the crucifixion speaking to Thomas Aquinas is depicted on this stained glass window in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick_Church_(Columbus,_Ohio)" title="Saint Patrick Church (Columbus, Ohio)">Saint Patrick Church (Columbus, Ohio)</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1272 Thomas took leave from the University of Paris when the Dominicans from his home province called upon him to establish a <i>studium generale</i> wherever he liked and staff it as he pleased. He chose to establish the institution in Naples, and moved there to take his post as regent master.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies20045_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies20045-53">[52]</a></sup> He took his time at Naples to work on the third part of the <i>Summa</i> while giving lectures on various religious topics. He also preached to the people of Naples every day in Lent, 1273. These <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Ninety-nine_Homilies_of_S._Thomas_Aquinas_Upon_the_Epistles_and_Gospels_for_Forty-nine_Sundays_of_the_Christian_Year" title="s:Ninety-nine Homilies of S. Thomas Aquinas Upon the Epistles and Gospels for Forty-nine Sundays of the Christian Year">sermons</a> on the Commandments, the Creed, the Our Father, and Hail Mary were very popular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas has been traditionally ascribed with the ability to <a href="/wiki/Levitation_(paranormal)" title="Levitation (paranormal)">levitate</a>. For example, <a href="/wiki/G._K._Chesterton" title="G. K. Chesterton">G. K. Chesterton</a> wrote that "His experiences included well-attested cases of levitation in ecstasy; and the Blessed Virgin appeared to him, comforting him with the welcome news that he would never be a Bishop."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (May 2020)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>It is traditionally held that on one occasion, in 1273 at the Dominican convent of Naples in the chapel of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Nicholas" title="Saint Nicholas">Saint Nicholas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> after <a href="/wiki/Matins" title="Matins">Matins</a>, Thomas lingered and was seen by the <a href="/wiki/Sacristan" title="Sacristan">sacristan</a> Domenic of Caserta to be levitating in prayer with tears before an icon of the crucified Christ. Christ said to Thomas, "You have written well of me, Thomas. What reward would you have for your labor?" Thomas responded, "Nothing but you, Lord."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_ToccoLe_Brun-Gouanvic1996_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_ToccoLe_Brun-Gouanvic1996-66">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKennedy1912_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKennedy1912-67">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 6 December 1273, another mystical experience took place. While he was celebrating Mass, he experienced an unusually long ecstasy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKennedy1912_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKennedy1912-67">[66]</a></sup> Because of what he saw, he abandoned his routine and refused to dictate to his <i>socius</i> <a href="/wiki/Reginald_of_Piperno" title="Reginald of Piperno">Reginald of Piperno</a>. When Reginald begged him to get back to work, Thomas replied: "Reginald, I cannot, because all that I have written seems like straw to me"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies19939_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies19939-68">[67]</a></sup> (<i>mihi videtur ut palea</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> As a result, the <i>Summa Theologica</i> would remain uncompleted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray201327Ch._10_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray201327Ch._10-70">[69]</a></sup> What exactly triggered Thomas's change in behavior is believed by some to have been some kind of supernatural experience of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP2_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEP2-71">[70]</a></sup> After taking to his bed, he did recover some strength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHealy20037_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHealy20037-72">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1054, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East-West_Schism" title="East-West Schism">Great Schism</a> had occurred between the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> in the West, and the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a>. Looking to find a way to reunite the two, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_X" title="Pope Gregory X">Pope Gregory X</a> convened the <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Lyon" title="Second Council of Lyon">Second Council of Lyon</a> to be held on 1 May 1274 and summoned Thomas to attend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENichols200218_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENichols200218-73">[72]</a></sup> At the meeting, Thomas's work for Pope Urban IV concerning the Greeks, <i>Contra errores graecorum</i>, was to be presented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHampden184846_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHampden184846-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>On his way to the council, riding on a donkey along the <a href="/wiki/Appian_Way" title="Appian Way">Appian Way</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENichols200218_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENichols200218-73">[72]</a></sup> he struck his head on the branch of a fallen tree and became seriously ill again. He was then quickly escorted to <a href="/wiki/Monte_Cassino" title="Monte Cassino">Monte Cassino</a> to convalesce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHealy20037_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHealy20037-72">[71]</a></sup> After resting for a while, he set out again, but stopped at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cistercian" title="Cistercian">Cistercian</a> <a href="/wiki/Fossanova_Abbey" title="Fossanova Abbey">Fossanova Abbey</a> after again falling ill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHealy20038_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHealy20038-75">[74]</a></sup> The monks nursed him for several days,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> and as he received his <a href="/wiki/Last_rites" title="Last rites">last rites</a> he prayed: "I have written and taught much about this very holy Body, and about the other sacraments in the faith of Christ, and about the Holy Roman Church, to whose correction I expose and submit everything I have written."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorrell2005292_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorrell2005292-77">[76]</a></sup> He died on 7 March 1274<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHealy20038_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHealy20038-75">[74]</a></sup> while giving commentary on the <a href="/wiki/Song_of_Songs" title="Song of Songs">Song of Songs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHampden184847_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHampden184847-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Condemnation_of_1277">Condemnation of 1277</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Condemnation of 1277">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Condemnations_of_1210%E2%80%931277" title="Condemnations of 1210–1277">Condemnations of 1210–1277</a></div>
<p>In 1277 <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Tempier" title="Étienne Tempier">Étienne Tempier</a>, the same bishop of Paris who had issued the condemnation of 1270, issued another more extensive condemnation. One aim of this condemnation was to clarify that God's absolute power transcended any principles of logic that <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> or <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a> might place on it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> More specifically, it contained a list of 219 propositions that the bishop had determined to violate the omnipotence of God, and included in this list were twenty Thomistic propositions. Their inclusion badly damaged Thomas's reputation for many years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKüng1994112_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKüng1994112-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">Divine Comedy</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante</a> sees the glorified soul of Thomas in the Heaven of the Sun with the other great exemplars of religious wisdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dante" title="Dante">Dante</a> asserts that Thomas died by poisoning, on the order of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_of_Anjou" title="Charles of Anjou">Charles of Anjou</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> Villani cites this belief,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anonimo_Fiorentino" title="Anonimo Fiorentino">Anonimo Fiorentino</a></i> describes the crime and its motive. But the historian <a href="/wiki/Ludovico_Antonio_Muratori" title="Ludovico Antonio Muratori">Ludovico Antonio Muratori</a> reproduces the account made by one of Thomas's friends, and this version of the story gives no hint of foul play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica-250_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-250-84">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas's theology had begun its rise to prestige. Two centuries later, in 1567, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_V" title="Pope Pius V">Pope Pius V</a> proclaimed St. Thomas Aquinas a <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_the_Church" title="Doctor of the Church">Doctor of the Church</a> and ranked his feast with those of the four great Latin fathers: <a href="/wiki/Ambrose" title="Ambrose">Ambrose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_I" title="Pope Gregory I">Gregory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica-250_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-250-84">[83]</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a>, Thomas had the honor of having his <i>Summa Theologiae</i> placed on the altar alongside the Bible and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decretals" title="Decretals">Decretals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKüng1994112_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKüng1994112-80">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mullady_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mullady-85">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his <a href="/wiki/Encyclical" title="Encyclical">encyclical</a> of 4 August 1879, <i><a href="/wiki/Aeterni_Patris" title="Aeterni Patris">Aeterni Patris</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_XIII" title="Pope Leo XIII">Pope Leo XIII</a> stated that Thomas Aquinas's theology was a definitive exposition of Catholic doctrine. Thus, he directed the clergy to take the teachings of Thomas as the basis of their theological positions. Leo XIII also decreed that all Catholic seminaries and universities must teach Thomas's doctrines, and where Thomas did not speak on a topic, the teachers were "urged to teach conclusions that were reconcilable with his thinking." In 1880, Saint Thomas Aquinas was declared patron of all Catholic educational establishments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica-250_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-250-84">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canonization">Canonization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Canonization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Canonization_of_Thomas_Aquinas" title="Canonization of Thomas Aquinas">Canonization of Thomas Aquinas</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Andrea_di_Bonaiuto._Santa_Maria_Novella_1366-7_fresco_0001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1251" data-file-width="1192" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Andrea_di_Bonaiuto._Santa_Maria_Novella_1366-7_fresco_0001.jpg/220px-Andrea_di_Bonaiuto._Santa_Maria_Novella_1366-7_fresco_0001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Andrea_di_Bonaiuto._Santa_Maria_Novella_1366-7_fresco_0001.jpg/330px-Andrea_di_Bonaiuto._Santa_Maria_Novella_1366-7_fresco_0001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Andrea_di_Bonaiuto._Santa_Maria_Novella_1366-7_fresco_0001.jpg/440px-Andrea_di_Bonaiuto._Santa_Maria_Novella_1366-7_fresco_0001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Andrea_di_Bonaiuto._Santa_Maria_Novella_1366-7_fresco_0001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Triumph of St. Thomas Aquinas</i>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Doctor Angelicus</i></span>, with saints and angels, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andrea_di_Bonaiuto" title="Andrea di Bonaiuto">Andrea di Bonaiuto</a>, 1366. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Santa_Maria_Novella" title="Basilica of Santa Maria Novella">Basilica of Santa Maria Novella</a>, fresco.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nef-jacobins-toulouse.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3168" data-file-width="4752" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Nef-jacobins-toulouse.jpg/220px-Nef-jacobins-toulouse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Nef-jacobins-toulouse.jpg/330px-Nef-jacobins-toulouse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Nef-jacobins-toulouse.jpg/440px-Nef-jacobins-toulouse.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nef-jacobins-toulouse.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The remains of Thomas Aquinas are buried in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Jacobins" title="Church of the Jacobins">Church of the Jacobins</a> in <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">Toulouse</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>When the <a href="/wiki/Devil%27s_advocate" title="Devil's advocate">devil's advocate</a> at his <a href="/wiki/Canonization" title="Canonization">canonization</a> process objected that there were no <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">miracles</a>, one of the cardinals answered, "<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Tot miraculis, quot articulis</i></span>"—"there are as many miracles (in his life) as articles (in his <a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica"><i>Summa</i></a>)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mullady_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mullady-85">[84]</a></sup> Fifty years after Thomas's death, on 18 July 1323, <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_XXII" title="Pope John XXII">Pope John XXII</a>, seated in <a href="/wiki/Avignon" title="Avignon">Avignon</a>, pronounced Thomas a <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saint</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHampden184854_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHampden184854-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>A monastery at Naples, near <a href="/wiki/Naples_Cathedral" title="Naples Cathedral">Naples Cathedral</a>, shows a cell in which he supposedly lived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica-250_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-250-84">[83]</a></sup> His remains were translated from Fossanova to the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Jacobins" title="Church of the Jacobins">Church of the Jacobins</a> in <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">Toulouse</a> on 28 January 1369. Between 1789 and 1974, they were held in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilique_de_Saint-Sernin,_Toulouse" title="Basilique de Saint-Sernin, Toulouse">Basilique de Saint-Sernin, Toulouse</a>. In 1974, they were returned to the Church of the Jacobins, where they have remained ever since.
</p><p>When he was canonized, his feast day was inserted in the <a href="/wiki/General_Roman_Calendar" title="General Roman Calendar">General Roman Calendar</a> for celebration on 7 March, the day of his death. Since this date commonly falls within <a href="/wiki/Lent" title="Lent">Lent</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mysterii_Paschalis" title="Mysterii Paschalis">1969 revision of the calendar</a> moved his <a href="/wiki/Memorial_(liturgy)" title="Memorial (liturgy)">memorial</a> to 28 January, the date of the translation of his relics to <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Jacobins" title="Church of the Jacobins">Church of the Jacobins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">Toulouse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas Aquinas is honored with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feast_day" title="Feast day">feast day</a> in some churches of the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Lesser_Festival_(Anglicanism)" title="Lesser Festival (Anglicanism)">Lesser Festival</a> on <a href="/wiki/January_28" title="January 28">28 January</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:046CupolaSPietro.jpg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/16px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/24px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/32px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg 2x" width="16"/></a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portal:Catholicism" title="Portal:Catholicism">Catholicism portal</a></li>
